        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.5b12620"><meta property="og:title" content="Highly Crystallized Na2CoFe(CN)6 with Suppressed Lattice Defects as Superior Cathode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/asset/images/acsami.5b12620.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Prussian blue and its analogues have received particular attention as superior cathodes for Na-ion batteries due to their potential 2-Na storage capacity (∼170 mAh g–1) and low cost. However, most of the Prussian blue compounds obtained from the conventional synthetic routes contain large amounts of Fe(CN)6 vacancies and coordinated water molecules, which leads to the collapse of cyano-bridged framework and serious deterioration of their Na-storage ability. Herein, we propose a facile citrate-assisted controlled crystallization method to obtain low-defect Prussian blue lattice with greatly improved Na-storage capacity and cycling stability. As an example, the as-prepared Na2CoFe(CN)6 nanocrystals demonstrate a reversible 2-Na storage reaction with a high specific capacity of 150 mAh g–1 and a ∼ 90% capacity retention over 200 cycles, possibly serving as a low cost and high performance cathode for Na-ion batteries. In particular, the synthetic strategy described in this work may be extended to other coordination-framework materials for a wide range of energy conversion and storage applications."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/asset/images/acsami.5b12620.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="931886b2fc4354b8-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="931886b2fc4354b8-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: 'ef7f3d5c-6d57-43c0-9d13-b1583f5fa0b3'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="931886b2fc4354b8-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

class="article--latest"><div class="figure-viewer"><div class="figure-viewer_row"><div class="figure-viewer_col loop-trap"><div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="figure-viewer_modal"><a <script nonce="931886b2fc4354b8-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Highly Crystallized Na&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;CoFe(CN)&lt;sub&gt;6&lt;/sub&gt; with Suppressed Lattice Defects as Superior Cathode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.5b12620", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Highly Crystallized Na&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;CoFe(CN)&lt;sub&gt;6&lt;/sub&gt; with Suppressed Lattice Defects as Superior Cathode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.5b12620", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Highly Crystallized Na&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;CoFe(CN)&lt;sub&gt;6&lt;/sub&gt; with Suppressed Lattice Defects as Superior Cathode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.5b12620", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Highly Crystallized Na&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;CoFe(CN)&lt;sub&gt;6&lt;/sub&gt; with Suppressed Lattice Defects as Superior Cathode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.5b12620", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Highly Crystallized Na&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;CoFe(CN)&lt;sub&gt;6&lt;/sub&gt; with Suppressed Lattice Defects as Superior Cathode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.5b12620", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Highly Crystallized Na&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;CoFe(CN)&lt;sub&gt;6&lt;/sub&gt; with Suppressed Lattice Defects as Superior Cathode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.5b12620", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Highly Crystallized Na&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;CoFe(CN)&lt;sub&gt;6&lt;/sub&gt; with Suppressed Lattice Defects as Superior Cathode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.5b12620", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Highly Crystallized Na&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;CoFe(CN)&lt;sub&gt;6&lt;/sub&gt; with Suppressed Lattice Defects as Superior Cathode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.5b12620", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Highly Crystallized Na&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;CoFe(CN)&lt;sub&gt;6&lt;/sub&gt; with Suppressed Lattice Defects as Superior Cathode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.5b12620", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

aria-describedby="about-article-metrics" class="article_aboutLink">Learn about these metrics</a><div data-db-target-of="aboutArticleMetrics" id="about-article-metrics" class="dropBlock__holder"><button class="articleMetrics_close"> <i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-close_thin"></i><span class="sr-only">close</span></button><p>Article Views are the COUNTER-compliant sum of full text article downloads since November 2008 (both PDF and HTML) across all institutions and individuals. Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="f_tgr1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/asset/images/medium/am-2015-12620u_0005.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/asset/images/large/am-2015-12620u_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.5b12620&amp;id=f_tgr1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Prussian blue and its analogues have received particular attention as superior cathodes for Na-ion batteries due to their potential 2-Na storage capacity (∼170 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) and low cost. However, most of the Prussian blue compounds obtained from the conventional synthetic routes contain large amounts of Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> vacancies and coordinated water molecules, which leads to the collapse of cyano-bridged framework and serious deterioration of their Na-storage ability. Herein, we propose a facile citrate-assisted controlled crystallization method to obtain low-defect Prussian blue lattice with greatly improved Na-storage capacity and cycling stability. As an example, the as-prepared Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> nanocrystals demonstrate a reversible 2-Na storage reaction with a high specific capacity of 150 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and a ∼ 90% capacity retention over 200 cycles, possibly serving as a low cost and high performance cathode for Na-ion batteries. In particular, the synthetic strategy described in this work may be extended to other coordination-framework materials for a wide range of energy conversion and storage applications.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D5393%26pageCount%3D7%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DXianyong%2BWu%252C%2BChenghao%2BWu%252C%2BCongxiao%2BWei%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D8%26issueNum%3D8%26contentID%3Dacsami.5b12620%26title%3DHighly%2BCrystallized%2BNa2CoFe%2528CN%25296%2Bwith%2BSuppressed%2BLattice%2BDefects%2Bas%2BSuperior%2BCathode%2BMaterial%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D7%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D5399%26publicationDate%3DMarch%2B2016" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2016 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292290&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Crystallization"> Crystallization </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290834&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Defects in solids"> Defects in solids </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290667&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Ions"> Ions </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291146&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Lattices"> Lattices </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">

                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Prussian+Blue+Framework&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Prussian%20blue%20framework">Prussian blue framework</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Lattice+Defects&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_lattice%20defects">lattice defects</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Controlled+Crystallization+Method&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_controlled%20crystallization%20method">controlled crystallization method</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium+Cobalt+Hexacyanoferrate&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium%20cobalt%20hexacyanoferrate">sodium cobalt hexacyanoferrate</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i1">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">With fast-growing demand for the integration of renewable energy sources such as solar and wind into the electric grids, large-scale electric energy storage (EES) technologies are urgently desired to suppress their inherent intermittency. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1, 2)</a> Among various types of rechargeable batteries, Li-ion batteries have been considered as most appealing candidate for grid-scale electric energy storage due to their high energy density and long cycle life; however, their limited lithium reserves on Earth and high cost might hinder their large-scale application. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref4 ref5" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3 ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 3 to 5">(3-5)</a> In pursuit of affordable electric storage batteries for EES applications, room-temperature Na-ion batteries appear to be an attractive alternative to their lithium counterparts because of the natural abundance and low cost of Na resources. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 6 and 7">(6, 7)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">To enable Na-ion technology, great efforts have been devoted to the development of suitable Na-insertion cathodes with not only adequate electrochemical capacity but also easy preparation. Until now, numerous host frameworks have been investigated as Na-storage cathodes, such as layered oxides, tunneled oxides, polyanion-type compounds and so on. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 8 to 13">(8-13)</a> Nevertheless, the strong binding of Na<sup>+</sup> ions in close-packed oxides makes them kinetically frustrated in the intercalation reaction, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9 ref10" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8 ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 8 to 10">(8-10)</a> while the heavy molar weight of large polyanions (XO<sub>4</sub>)<sup><i>n</i>−</sup> (X = P, S, As, Si, etc.) restricts the reversible capacity to less than 120 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12 ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref11 ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 11 to 13">(11-13)</a> Recently, Prussian blue analogues Na<sub>2</sub>M[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] (M = Mn, Fe, Co, Ni, Cu, etc.) have received particular attention as alternative Na-storage cathodes, due to their three-dimensional open frameworks, potential two-electron redox capacity (up to 170 mAh/g with 2 Na<sup>+</sup> storage) and fast intercalation kinetics. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 14 to 17">(14-17)</a> Generally, Prussian blue analogues can be easily synthesized via a simple coprecipitation reaction of transition-metal M<sup>n+</sup> cations and hexacyanoferrate Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub><sup>3–/4–</sup> anions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 18 to 21">(18-21)</a> For instance, Goodenough et al. first introduced a KMFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> family (M = Mn, Fe, Co, Ni, Cu, Zn) as nonaqueous sodium ion cathodes early in 2012. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> However, all these materials have reversible capacities less than 100 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and low Coulombic efficiencies of 60–80%, which are insufficient for practical battery applications. The latest research disclosed that the electrochemical performance of PB is closely related to its intrinsic crystal structure. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 23 to 29">(23-29)</a> It is known that during the conventional rapid precipitation process, large amounts of Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> vacancies occupied by coordinated water will exist in the crystal framework, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 30 and 31">(30, 31)</a> leading to an actual chemical formula of Na<sub>2–<i>x</i></sub>M[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>1–<i>y</i></sub>·□<sub><i>y</i></sub>·zH<sub>2</sub>O, in which □ stands for the Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> vacancies; 0 &lt; <i>x</i> &lt; 2; 0 &lt; <i>y</i> &lt; 1, as illustrated in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="1">1</a>. The presence of Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> vacancies will severely deteriorate the battery performance for the following reasons: (1) the increase of Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> vacancies will reduce the Na content in the lattice due to charge balance and introduce more water molecules into PB framework, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 30 and 31">(30, 31)</a> thus decreasing the initial charge capacity and causing more side reactions brought about by H<sub>2</sub>O decomposition; <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22 ref26" aria-label="reference 22 and 26">(22, 26)</a> (2) the crystal water molecules may compete with Na<sup>+</sup> ions to accommodate the interstitial spaces, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23 ref26" aria-label="reference 23 and 26">(23, 26)</a> therefore blocking off the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion reactions and decreasing the capacity utilization of PB compounds; (3) the randomly distributed Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> vacancies makes the PB frameworks fragile and vulnerable to collapse during the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction reactions, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 23 to 27">(23-27)</a> thus leading to the structural instability and poor cycle life.</div><div class="NLM_p">Therefore, it is of great importance to control the crystallization process so as to fabricate low-defect and well-defined PB frameworks and thus boost their ion storage capability. For instance, our group <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 23 and 24">(23, 24)</a> and Guo et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 25 and 26">(25, 26)</a> reported on a slow chemical precipitation method to fabricate low-defect FeFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> and Na<sub>0.61</sub>Fe[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.94</sub> nanocrystals that can realize a stable 2Na-storage reaction for hundreds of cycles with a high Coulombic efficiency of ∼100%. Cui’s group <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> recently succeeded in crystallizing almost perfectly stoichiometric Na<sub>1.96</sub>Mn[Mn(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.99</sub> □<sub>0.01</sub> 2 H<sub>2</sub>O by using a large excess of sodium in solution during the synthesis, and achieved a surprisingly high capacity of 209 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, nicely proving the effectiveness of minimizing the fraction of vacancy sites for advancing PB chemistry.</div><div class="NLM_p">Herein, we propose a facile citrate-assisted controlled crystallization method to synthesize low-defect and highly crystallized Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> nanocrystals. UV–vis spectrum confirms the chelating effects of citrate with Co ions, while in situ UV–vis spectral experiment proves the significantly decreased crystallization kinetics. Benefiting from its suppressed Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> defects and high crystalline, this material demonstrates highly reversible 2-Na storage reactions with a high specific capacity of 150 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and strong cycle stability with ∼90% capacity retention over 200 cycles. Such a synthetic method to obtain high-quality PB frameworks could be extended to a wide range of coordination-framework materials for electric energy conversion and storage applications.</div><figure id="sch1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Scheme 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/asset/images/medium/am-2015-12620u_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/asset/images/large/am-2015-12620u_0004.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><div class="title2">Scheme 1. Schematic Crystal Structures of Prussian Blue Frameworks</div></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/asset/images/large/am-2015-12620u_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.5b12620&amp;id=sch1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div><p class="last"></p><p><span class="fn-label">Scheme a</span>An intact Na<sub>2</sub>M<sup>II</sup>[Fe<sup>II</sup>(CN)<sub>6</sub>] framework without structural defects. The M ions are 6-fold coordinated to the nitrogen atoms of the CN ligands, whereas Fe ions are octahedrally neighbored with the carbon atoms of the CN ligands, forming a three-dimensional polymeric framework with large interstitial spaces. The guests (Na<sup>+</sup> ions and zeolitic water molecules) occupy the interstitial “A” sites at the center of every eight sub-cubes. Zeolitic water molecules are omitted for clarity.</p><p class="last"><span class="fn-label">Scheme b</span>An ideally defective NaM<sup>II</sup>[Fe<sup>II</sup>(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.75</sub>•<sub>□0</sub>.<sub>25</sub> framework with 25% Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> vacancies existing in each unit cell. In this case, The M ions neighboring to vacancies are coordinated by water molecules whose total number in the unit is six. A real Prussian blue product would fall in between these two ideal crystal structures depending on the synthesis route.</p><p></p></figure></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">In general, traditional synthesis of Prussian blue compounds Na<sub>2</sub>M[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] is based on a direct precipitation reaction of M<sup>2+</sup> cations and [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>4–</sup> anions in aqueous solution, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a (I). Due to the extremely small solubility product constant of Na<sub>2</sub>Co[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>], <i>K</i><sub>sp</sub> = 1.8 × 10<sup>–15</sup>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> the nucleation and growth stage occurs immediately and simultaneously, thus leading to irregularly shaped and randomly aggregated nanoparticles (∼50 nm) with large amounts of Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> defects and coordinated water in the lattice (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="1">1</a>b). To obtain a more well-defined crystal lattice (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="1">1</a>a), we adopted a citrate-chelating method to control the crystallization process as illustrated in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a (II). The Co<sup>2+</sup> ions are first coordinated with citrate ions to form cobalt-citrate chelate and then coprecipitated with hexacyanoferrate ions in aqueous solution. Due to the very strong complex ability of citrate ions (Co<sup>2+</sup>·Citrate<sup>3–</sup>, log <i>K</i><sub>1</sub> = 12.5), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 33 and 34">(33, 34)</a> the cobalt-citrate chelate acts as a reservoir to slowly release Co<sup>2+</sup> ions, which will react with hexacyanoferrate ions to form initial nucleuses. The negatively charged citrate ions may even absorb on these nucleuses surfaces, thus suppressing the growth rate and inhibiting crystal aggregation. Eventually, the gradual and sustained release of Co ions will enable these nucleuses to grow into well-shaped and monodispersed Prussian blue nanocubes (∼600 nm) with greatly suppressed Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> defects and structural imperfection.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/asset/images/medium/am-2015-12620u_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/asset/images/large/am-2015-12620u_0007.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Schematic illustration of the conventional coprecipitation method (I) and the citrate-assisted controlled crystallization process (II) for the synthesis of Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub>; (b) and (c) UV–vis absorption spectra of citrate, Co<sup>2+</sup>, citrate–Co<sup>2+</sup>, Na<sub>4</sub>Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub>; (d) In situ UV–vis spectral monitoring of the crystallization kinetics of Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> synthesized with or without the addition of citrate ions as slow-release chelator. The absorption intensity is recorded at 370 nm, which is characteristic of Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub>. The inset is a magnified picture of the initial nucleation stage.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/asset/images/large/am-2015-12620u_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.5b12620&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">UV–vis spectrometry was carried out to reveal the interactions between citrate ions and Co ions. As can be seen in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b, the blank Co<sup>2+</sup> solution showed a maximal absorption at 510 nm wavelength, while pure citrate ions solution nearly had no absorption signal in this area. After the addition of citrate ions into Co<sup>2+</sup> solution, the maximal absorption peak slightly shifted to lower a wavelength at 506 nm with obviously increased absorption intensity, indicating the coordination effect of citrate with Co ions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> In situ UV–vis spectroscopy was used to monitor the crystallization kinetics of Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> with or without the presence of citrate as chelating agent. The absorption intensity at 370 nm wavelength was recorded as a function of reaction time, which reflects the change in the relative content of Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> product as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d, once the Co<sup>2+</sup> and Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub><sup>4–</sup> ions were mixed together in the absence of citrate ions, the absorption intensity reached its maximal value within a very short time (less than 10 s) and then remained unchanged, indicating the ultrafast crystallization process in a time scale of seconds. On the contrary, in the presence of citrate ions, the absorption intensity preserved at a very low value during 0–100 s, which could be ascribed to the initial nucleation stage. Then, it increased incrementally from 100 to 1000 s, which might correspond to the subsequent crystal growth process. Even after 3000 s, the intensity was still lower than that of control experiment. These results suggested that the crystallization speed could be significantly slowed down by the addition of citrate ions as slow-release chelator, and thus a highly crystallized Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> sample with reduced structural defects can be obtained. It is also found that the developed synthesis method is general and can be extended to prepare other Prussian blue analogues (e.g., Na<sub>2</sub>NiFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>CuFe(CN)<sub>6</sub>) with high crystallinity and suppressed structural defects.</div><div class="NLM_p">On the basis of the ICP-AES spectroscopy and elemental analysis (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/suppl_file/am5b12620_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>), we can express the precise chemical composition of the as-synthesized Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> material as Na<sub>1.85</sub>Co[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.99</sub>·□<sub>0.01</sub>·1.9H<sub>2</sub>O, whereas the control sample got in the absence of citrate ions is Na<sub>1.32</sub>Co[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.88</sub>·□<sub>0.12</sub>·3.6H<sub>2</sub>O. The significant suppression of Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> defects from conventional 12% to 1% (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/suppl_file/am5b12620_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>) enables more Na<sup>+</sup> ions (1.85 Na<sup>+</sup>) to enter into the lattice for charge counterbalance, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> which is favorable for the improvement of initial charge capacity. The inhibition of Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> vacancies can be also reflected by the reduction of water content in the lattice. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/suppl_file/am5b12620_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>, Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) revealed only 10% weight loss in the temperature interval 200–250 °C, corresponding to 1.9 H<sub>2</sub>O per Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> unit. Besides, this low-defect material demonstrates greatly improved thermal stability than that of control sample (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/suppl_file/am5b12620_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>), suggesting a more stable and well-defined crystal structure. The infrared spectrum of the Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> sample given in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/suppl_file/am5b12620_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a> shows a singlet peak at 2080 cm<sup>–1</sup>, which can be attributed to the stretching vibration of Fe<sup>2+</sup>-CN-Co<sup>2+</sup>, agreeing well with its chemical formula. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a></div><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a> shows the crystalline structure and morphological features of the Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> material. All the X-ray diffraction lines shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a are sharp and strong, indicating high crystallinity of the Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> sample. Careful comparison with the standard diffraction pattern of Prussian blue Fe<sub>4</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>3</sub> (JCPDS No. 52-1907) demonstrated a rhombohedral distortion from traditional face-centered cubic structure, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37 ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37 ref38" aria-label="reference 37 and 38">(37, 38)</a> as seen by its (220), (420), (440), and (620) diffraction peaks splitting into almost equal intensities. Very recently, similar phase transition has been also observed in other Prussian blue frameworks, such as Na<sub>1.72</sub>Mn[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.99</sub> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> and Na<sub>1.92</sub>FeFe(CN)<sub>6</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> where large accommodation of Na<sup>+</sup> ions (in our case, 1.85 Na<sup>+</sup>) causes an expansion of elementary cell and a decrease in symmetry. In contrast, the control Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> sample with insufficient Na<sup>+</sup> content (only 1.32 Na<sup>+</sup>) in its lattice shows a typical face-centered cubic structure, further confirming such Na<sup>+</sup>-induced phase transformation phenomenon (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/suppl_file/am5b12620_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>). The rhombohedral structure can be clearly visualized in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b. The SEM image in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c reveals the Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> sample appears as well-dispersed, uniformly sized and regularly shaped nanocubes with a narrow size distribution of ∼600 nm, which probably benefited from the as-mentioned controlled crystallization mechanism. Energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) mapping images in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d,e confirm the very uniform dispersion of Na, Co, Fe, C, N, and O element in the bulk Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> sample, further suggesting its high purity and crystal integrity.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/asset/images/medium/am-2015-12620u_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/asset/images/large/am-2015-12620u_0001.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Physical properties of the as-prepared Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> sample: (a) XRD patterns; (b) crystal structure; (c) SEM image; and (d and e) energy dispersive spectroscopy mapping images.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/asset/images/large/am-2015-12620u_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.5b12620&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Electrochemical Na-storage performance of the Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> electrodes were characterized by cyclic voltammetry (CV) and galvanostatic charge–discharge tests. As displayed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a, the main CV curves demonstrate two pairs of sharp and symmetric oxidation/redox peaks at 3.42/3.12 V and 3.89/3.73 V, indicating the 2-Na reaction mechanism of the Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> electrode. Taking into account the difference of coordination environment of Fe and Co atoms, the CV peaks at lower and higher potentials can be attributed to the nitrogen-coordinated Co<sup>2+</sup>/Co<sup>3+</sup> couple and carbon-coordinated Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> couple, respectively, as will be confirmed by XPS tests in subsequent context. Surprisingly, the Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> redox couple (∼3.8 V, E<sub>redox</sub> = (<i>E</i><sub>anodic</sub> + E<sub>cathodic</sub>)/2) in Co–Fe Prussian blue framework shows a significant deviation from the standard electrode potential (3.07 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) of Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub><sup>4–</sup>/Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub><sup>3–</sup>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> and is even higher than that of Co<sup>2+</sup>/Co<sup>3+</sup> couple, which can been interpreted as a strong charge-spin–lattice coupling effect in the low-defect Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> framework. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38 ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38 ref41" aria-label="reference 38 and 41">(38, 41)</a> Obviously, this coupling effect is beneficial for the Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> electrode to raise its working potential.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/asset/images/medium/am-2015-12620u_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/asset/images/large/am-2015-12620u_0002.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Electrochemical performance of the Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> electrode: (a) typical CV curve measured at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>; (b) charge–discharge profiles at a current density of 10 mA g<sup>–1</sup>; (c) rate performance; and (d) long-term cycle performance at current density of 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/asset/images/large/am-2015-12620u_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.5b12620&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b shows the charge–discharge profiles of the as-prepared Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> electrode between 2.0 and 4.1 V at a current density of 10 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. In accord with the CV curves, the Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> electrode exhibits two distinguishable potential plateaus at 3.8 and 3.2 V, corresponding to the successive redox reactions of the Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> and Co<sup>2+</sup>/Co<sup>3+</sup> couples, respectively. The high Na<sup>+</sup> content (1.85) in Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> lattice is reflected in a charge capacity of 153 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> in the first cycle, with initial Coulombic efficiency as high as 98%. On the basis of its chemical formula, the specific discharge capacity (150 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) corresponds to a 95% utilization of its theoretical capacity (158 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for Na<sub>1.85</sub>Co[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.99</sub>·1.9H<sub>2</sub>O). Such high capacity utilization and high Coulombic efficiency are most likely resulted from its perfect framework with suppressed Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> defects, which provides sufficient interstitial sites for reversible Na insertion. To understand the influence of Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> vacancies on the electrochemical performance of PB framework, we compared the typical charge/discharge profiles of the two Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> electrodes synthesized with and without the assistance of citrate ions. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/suppl_file/am5b12620_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>, both Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> electrodes exhibit two distinct discharge plateaus at 3.8 and 3.2 V respectively, indicating a very similar Na-insertion reaction mechanism. However, careful comparison of their Na-storage properties demonstrates that the control sample exhibits greater voltage polarization (215 mV) between charge and discharge plateaus, delivers much lower discharge capacity (115 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) and demonstrates lower Coulombic efficiency (∼95%) than that of as-prepared sample (144 mV, 150 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, and ∼99%). By calculating the capacity contributions of Fe and Co redox centers, we found that the capacity difference mainly originates from the capacity utilization of Fe redox center (28 vs 60 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) at higher reaction potential, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/suppl_file/am5b12620_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4b</a>, and this should be closely related to the amount of Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> vacancies in the PB lattice. It has been estimated that the Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> vacancies may impede the e<sup>–</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> transportation along the Fe-CN-Co framework, and increase the polarization between charge/discharge plateaus. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> Besides, the coordinated water molecules will compete with Na<sup>+</sup> to occupy the interstitial sites, thus blocking off the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion reaction. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> Hence, the suppression of Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> vacancies can greatly enhance the Na-storage ability of Fe atoms and decrease the polarization between charge and discharge.</div><div class="NLM_p">The high capacity and high redox potential of the as-prepared Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> electrode contributes to an energy density as high as 510 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup>, which is comparable to that of cathodes for state-of-art Li-ion battery, such as, LiMn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (120 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, 480 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup>) and LiCoO<sub>2</sub> (137 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, 550 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref2 ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref2 ref42" aria-label="reference 2 and 42">(2, 42)</a> Furthermore, the discharge capacity above 3.0 V comprises ∼93% of its whole Na-insertion capacity, offering great benefit for practical implementation of Na-ion batteries. In the subsequent cycles (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b), its redox capacity remains stable at ∼150 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, indicating the structural stability of Fe-CN-Co framework during charge and discharge process. In addition to its high capacity and high redox potential, the as prepared Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> electrode also demonstrates good rate capability and long-term cyclability. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c, the reversible capacity is 148, 135, 128, 88, and 60 at the current density of 20, 50, 100, 200, and 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. This good rate capability is mainly due to its three-dimension open framework structure with large interstitial space for fast Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction reactions. The long-term cycle performance is evaluated at a moderate current density of 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> as given in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d. The reversible capacity decreases slightly from 128 to 114 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> over 200 cycles, corresponding to a capacity retention of ∼90%. In strong contrast, the control Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> sample synthesized via traditional coprecipitation method shows an enormous performance degradation with only 30% of its initial capacity retained after 200 cycles. Therefore, the superior cycle stability of as-prepared Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> definitely results from its highly crystallized and low-defect lattice, which facilitates highly reversible 2-Na storage reactions. The structural (XRD) and morphological features (SEM) of Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> electrode could be well preserved after 200 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/suppl_file/am5b12620_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>), further confirming its stable and robust framework during the repeated Na insertion and extraction process.</div><div class="NLM_p">To clarify the phase transition and redox-active site during the 2-Na reaction process, ex situ XRD tests (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>) and XPS analyses (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/suppl_file/am5b12620_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>) were carried out at different charge/discharge states. When this Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> electrode was charged from +2.0 V state to +3.4 V (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a), the (2<span class="overline">2</span>0) and (220) XRD doublets merged into a single peak at ∼25°, indicating the phase transition from rhombohedral lattice to a cubic one. As mentioned above, this phase transformation phenomenon is probably induced by the Na<sup>+</sup> extraction from the lattice to form a Na-deficient framework. At the same time, XPS test (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/suppl_file/am5b12620_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>) confirmed the valence change of Co ions from +2 to +3, while Fe ions remained at +2 state. When charged from +3.4 V to a terminal +4.1 V, the whole XRD lines remained unchanged, suggesting no phase change at deep charge. This stage was related to the oxidization of Fe ions from +2 to +3 state, as revealed by XPS analysis. As mentioned above, such positive shift of Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub><sup>4–</sup>/Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub><sup>3–</sup> couple from its standard electrode potential (3.07 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> is quite anomalous, and is most likely derived from the hybridization effect between low-spin Co<sup>3+</sup> and low-spin Fe<sup>2+</sup> atoms along cyanide-bridged ligands as already confirmed by ab initio band calculation by Moritomo et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38 ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38 ref41" aria-label="reference 38 and 41">(38, 41)</a> Once reversed to discharge, the XRD patterns of Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> electrode restored its initial state, indicating the reversible structural transformation from cubic phase to rhombohedral one. On the whole, the Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> electrode undergoes a reversible 2-Na reaction mechanism: rhombohedral Na<sub>2</sub>Co<sup>II</sup>Fe<sup>II</sup>(CN)<sub>6</sub> ↔ cubic NaCo<sup>III</sup>Fe<sup>II</sup>(CN)<sub>6</sub> ↔ cubic Co<sup>III</sup>Fe<sup>III</sup>(CN)<sub>6</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a></div><figure id="fig4" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/asset/images/medium/am-2015-12620u_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/asset/images/large/am-2015-12620u_0003.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Na storage mechanism of the Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> electrode: (a) A typical charge/discharge profile of the Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> electrode at different depths: (point a) initial state +2.0 V, (point b) charged to +3.4 V, (point c) charged to +4.1 V, (point d) discharged to +3.4 V, and (point e) discharged to +2.0 V; (b) ex situ XRD patterns at selected states.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/asset/images/large/am-2015-12620u_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.5b12620&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">Conclusion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, we developed a facile citrate-assisted controlled crystallization method to fabricate low-defect and well-defined Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> frameworks. In situ UV–vis spectral experiment proves the significantly decreased crystallization kinetics in the presence of citrate ions as the slow-release chelating agent. Owing to its high crystallinity and suppressed Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> defects, the as-prepared Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> material exhibits highly reversible 2-Na reactions with high capacity of 150 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and superior long-term cyclability of ∼90% over 200 cycles, showing appealing promise as Na-rich high-capacity cathode for Na ions applications. Moreover, this synthetic strategy may be extended to other coordination-framework materials for a wide range of energy conversion and storage applications.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i5">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec4_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Synthesis of Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> samples</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The as-prepared Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> sample was synthesized by a citrate-assisted controlled crystallization method at room temperature. Typically, 5 mmol CoCl<sub>2</sub> and 5 mmol trisodium citrate were dissolved into 50 mL deionized water to form a 0.1 mol L<sup>–1</sup> citrate<sup>3–</sup>–Co<sup>2+</sup> chelate solution. Then, this solution (50 mL) and 0.1 mol L<sup>–1</sup> Na<sub>4</sub>Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> solution (50 mL) were simultaneously added dropwise to 100 mL of deionized water for their coprecipitation reaction. The reaction solution was stirred for 12 h after complete addition. The resulting precipitates were filtered, washed with water and ethanol several times, and finally dried under vacuum at 60 °C for 24 h. As comparison, the control Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> samples were prepared similarly except for the addition of citrate ions.</div></div><div id="sec4_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Physical Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The chemical compositions of two Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> samples (Na<sub>2–<i>x</i></sub>Co[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>1–<i>y</i></sub>•□<sub><i>y</i></sub>, □ stands for the Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> vacancy, 0 &lt; <i>y</i> &lt; 1) were determined by ICP-AES calibration of Na, Co, and Fe contents and elemental analysis (Elementar Analysen systeme GmbH, Germany) of C and N elements. The water contents in the Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> materials were characterized by thermogravimetric measurement (TA Instruments, New Castle, DE) in nitrogen atmosphere at a scan rate of 10 °C/min. The lattice structures of the samples were characterized by XRD technique on a Shimadzu XRD-6000 diffractometer with Cu Kα radiation. The crystal structure was drawn by Diamond software. The morphologies of these samples were observed on Scanning Electron Microscope (Sirion 2000, FEI). FT-IR spectra were recorded on a NICOLET AVATAR 360 FT-IR spectrometer with KBr pellets. UV–vis spectra were recorded using a UV-3100 UV–vis–NIR recording spectrophotometer made by SHIMADZU Company. The in situ crystallization process was measured under the kinetics mode of UV–vis spectrophotometer, and the characteristic emission wavelength was set to be 370 nm. Upon mixing the two reaction solutions into the quartz absorption cell, the cell was immediately transferred into the sample holder of the spectrophotometer. The X-ray photoelectron spectra of the Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> samples were measured on a Thermo Scientific spectrometer (ESCLAB 250 Xi) equipped with an Al Kα achromatic X-ray source (1486.68 eV). The binding energies of all the elements were calibrated with respect to the carbon (284.6 eV).</div></div><div id="sec4_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> Electrochemical Measurement</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The electrochemical measurements of the Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> electrodes were examined by 2016 type coin cells using Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> cathode as a working electrode and a Na disk as reference and counter electrode. The working electrode was prepared by rolling a mixture of 70 wt % as-prepared materials, 20 wt % conducting graphite, and 10 wt % polytetrafluoroethylene into a thin film with a thickness of ∼100 um. The mass loading of the active material within the film was about 4 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. The electrolyte was 1.0 mol L<sup>–1</sup> NaClO<sub>4</sub> in ethylene carbonate/diethyl carbonate (EC/DEC, 50:50% vol) solution. All the cells were assembled in a glovebox with water/oxygen content lower than 1 ppm and tested at room temperature. The galvanostatic charge–discharge tests were performed on a LAND cycler (Wuhan Kingnuo Electronic Co., China). Cyclic voltammetric measurements were carried out at a scanning rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> using a CHI 660a electrochemical workstation (ChenHua Instruments Co., China).</div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.5b12620" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.5b12620</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Chemical compositions, TG curves, infrared spectra, XRD patterns, SEM images, electrochemical properties of these two Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> materials. (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/suppl_file/am5b12620_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/suppl_file/am5b12620_si_001.pdf">am5b12620_si_001.pdf (1.33 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.5b12620" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The increasing interest in energy storage for the grid can be attributed to multiple factors, including the capital costs of managing peak demands, the investments needed for grid reliability, and the integration of renewable energy sources.  Although existing energy storage is dominated by pumped hydroelec., there is the recognition that battery systems can offer a no. of high-value opportunities, provided that lower costs can be obtained.  The battery systems reviewed here include sodium-sulfur batteries that are com. available for grid applications, redox-flow batteries that offer low cost, and lithium-ion batteries whose development for com. electronics and elec. vehicles is being applied to grid storage.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1038/451652a"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Armand, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tarascon, J.
