        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Energy prodn. and storage have become key issues concerning our welfare in daily life.  Present challenges for batteries are twofold.  In the first place, the increasing demand for powering systems of portable electronic devices and zero-emission vehicles stimulates research towards high energy and high voltage systems.  In the second place, low cost batteries are required in order to advance towards smart elec. grids that integrate discontinuous energy flow from renewable sources, optimizing the performance of clean energy sources.  Na-ion batteries can be the key for the second point, because of the huge availability of sodium, its low price and the similarity of both Li and Na insertion chemistries.  In spite of the lower energy d. and voltage of Na-ion based technologies, they can be focused on applications where the wt. and footprint requirement is less drastic, such as elec. grid storage.  Much work has to be done in the field of Na-ion in order to catch up with Li-ion technol.  Cathodic and anodic materials must be optimized, and new electrolytes will be the key point for Na-ion success.  This review will gather the up-to-date knowledge about Na-ion battery materials, with the aim of providing a wide view of the systems that have already been explored and a starting point for the new research on this battery technol.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref4" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1039/c3ee40847g"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Pan, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, Y.-S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, L.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Room-Temperature Stationary Sodium-Ion Batteries for Large-Scale Electric Energy Storage</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Energy Environ.                         cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Room-temperature stationary sodium-ion batteries for large-scale electric energy storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Pan, Huilin; Hu, Yong-Sheng; Chen, Liquan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries have attracted great attention particularly in large-scale elec. energy storage applications for renewable energy and smart grid because of the huge abundant sodium resources and low cost.  In this article, a variety of electrode materials including cathodes and anodes as well as electrolytes for room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries are briefly reviewed.  We compare the difference in storage behavior between Na and Li in their analogous electrodes and summarize the sodium storage mechanisms in the available electrode materials.  This review also includes some new results from our group and our thoughts on developing new materials.  Some perspectives and directions on designing better materials for practical applications are pointed out based on knowledge from the literature and our experience.  Through this extensive literature review, the search for suitable electrode and electrolyte materials for stationary sodium-ion batteries is still challenging.  However, after intensive research efforts, we believe that low-cost, long-life and room-temp. sodium-ion batteries would be promising for applications in large-scale energy storage system in the near future.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref5" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1021/cr500192f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yabuuchi, N.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kubota, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dahbi, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Komaba, S.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Research Development on Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Chem.                         cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrospun Sb/C Fibers for a Stable and Fast Sodium-Ion Battery Anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhu, Yujie; Han, Xiaogang; Xu, Yunhua; Liu, Yihang; Zheng, Shiyou; Xu, Kang; Hu, Liangbing; Wang, Chunsheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">6378-6386</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ANCAC3</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1936-0851</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered a top alternative to Li-ion batteries (LIBs) for large-scale renewable energy storage units due to their low cost and the abundance of Na-bearing precursors in the earth's mineral deposits.  However, the development of anode materials for SIBs to date was mainly limited to carbonaceous materials with minimal research devoted to high capacity alloy-based materials.  An Sb/C electrode with ∼30 nm Sb nanoparticles (NPs) uniformly encapsulated in interconnecting 1-dimensional (1D) 400 nm C fibers (denoted as SbNP@C) was fabricated using a simple and scalable electrospinning method.  This binder-free, current collector-free SbNP@C electrode demonstrated high capacity and stable long-term cycling performance at various current densities.  The SbNP@C electrode showed an initial total capacity of 422 mA-h/gelectrode and retained 350 mA-h/gelectrode after 300 deep charge-discharge cycles under 100 mA/gSb.  Also, because of the efficient 1-dimensional Na-ion transport pathway and the highly conductive network of SbNP@C, the electrode preserved high overall capacities even when cycled at high currents, extending its usability to high power applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref8" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1038/nmat3309"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yabuuchi, N.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kajiyama, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Iwatate, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Nishikawa, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hitomi, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Okuyama, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Usui, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yamada, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Komaba, S.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">P2-Type Na<sub>x</sub>[Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> Made from Earth-Abundant Elements for Rechargeable Na batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Nat. Mn- and Fe-based cathode materials, such as LiMn2O4 and LiFePO4, are used in large-scale batteries for elec. vehicles.  Mn and Fe are abundant elements in the Earth's crust, but Li is not.  In contrast to Li, Na is an attractive charge carrier from elemental abundance.  Recently, some layered materials, where Na can be electrochem. and reversibly extd./inserted, have been reported.                         cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A New High-Energy Cathode for a Na-Ion Battery with Ultrahigh Stability</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Park, Young-Uk; Seo, Dong-Hwa; Kwon, Hyung-Soon; Kim, Byoungkook; Kim, Jongsoon; Kim, Haegyeom; Kim, Inkyung; Yoo, Han-Ill; Kang, Kisuk</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Large-scale elec. energy storage is a key enabler for the use of renewable energy.  Recently, the room-temp. Na-ion battery has been re-highlighted as an alternative low-cost technol. for this application.  However, significant challenges such as energy d. and long-term stability must be addressed.  Herein, we introduce a novel cathode material, Na1.5VPO4.8F0.7, for Na-ion batteries.  This new material provides an energy d. of ∼600 Wh kg-1, the highest value among cathodes, originating from both the multielectron redox reaction (1.2 e- per formula unit) and the high potential (∼3.8 V vs Na+/Na) of the tailored vanadium redox couple (V3.8+/V5+). The open crystal framework with two-dimensional Na diffusional pathways leads to low activation barriers for Na diffusion, enabling excellent rate capability.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Aq. Na-ion batteries have the competitive advantages of being low costing and possessing inherent safety, and they are particularly suitable for large-scale energy-storage applications.  However, the development of this new battery system is hindered by the lack of suitable Na hosts with sufficiently high capacity and cycle life.  Herein, we report vacancy-free Na2CoFe(CN)6 nanocubes synthesized by a controlled crystn. reaction and reveal their use as a new aq. cathode with reversible Na-storage behavior.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The elec. power grid faces a growing need for large-scale energy storage over a wide range of time scales due to costly short-term transients, frequency regulation, and load balancing.  The durability, high power, energy efficiency, and low cost needed for grid-scale storage pose substantial challenges for conventional battery technol.  Here, we demonstrate insertion/extn. of sodium and potassium ions in a low-strain nickel hexacyanoferrate electrode material for at least five thousand deep cycles at high current densities in inexpensive aq. electrolytes.  Its open-framework structure allows retention of 66% of the initial capacity even at a high (41.7C) rate.  At low current densities, its round trip energy efficiency reaches 99%.  This low-cost material is readily synthesized in bulk quantities.  The long cycle life, high power, good energy efficiency, safety, and inexpensive prodn. method make nickel hexacyanoferrate an attractive candidate for use in large-scale batteries to support the elec. grid.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref21" class="refNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit21" data-doi="10.1021/nn204666v"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wessells, C.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The elec. energy grid has a growing need for energy storage to address short-term transients, frequency regulation, and load leveling.  Though electrochem. energy storage devices such as batteries offer an attractive soln., current com. battery technol. cannot provide adequate power, and cycle life, and energy efficiency at a sufficiently low cost.  Copper hexacyanoferrate and nickel hexacyanoferrate, two open framework materials with the Prussian Blue structure, were recently shown to offer ultralong cycle life and high-rate performance when operated as battery electrodes in safe, inexpensive aq. sodium ion and potassium ion electrolytes.  In this report, it is demonstrated that the reaction potential of copper-nickel alloy hexacyanoferrate nanoparticles may be tuned by controlling the ratio of copper to nickel in these materials.  X-ray diffraction, TEM energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy, and galvanostatic electrochem. cycling of copper-nickel hexacyanoferrate reveal that copper and nickel form a fully miscible soln. at particular sites in the framework without perturbing the structure.  This allows copper-nickel hexacyanoferrate to reversibly intercalate sodium and potassium ions for over 2000 cycles with capacity retentions of 100 and 91%, resp.  The ability to precisely tune the reaction potential of copper-nickel hexacyanoferrate without sacrificing cycle life will allow the development of full cells that utilize the entire electrochem. stability window of aq. sodium and potassium ion electrolytes.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref22"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref22" class="refNumLink">22</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit22" data-doi="10.1039/c2cc31777j"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cheng, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Goodenough, J.                         cas_cit25R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">25</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium iron hexacyanoferrate with high Na content as a Na-rich cathode material for Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">You, Ya; Yu, Xiqian; Yin, Yaxia; Nam, Kyung-Wan; Guo, Yu-Guo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Research</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">8</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">117-128</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Springer GmbH</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Owing to the worldwide abundance and low-cost of Na, room-temp. Na-ion batteries are emerging as attractive energy storage systems for large-scale grids.  Increasing the Na content in cathode materials is one of the effective ways to achieve high energy d.  Prussian blue and its analogs (PBAs) are promising Na-rich cathode materials since they can theor. store two Na+ ions per formula unit.  However, increasing the Na content in PBAs cathode materials remains a major challenge.  Here we show that sodium iron hexacyanoferrate with high Na content can be obtained by simply controlling the reducing agent and reaction atm. during synthesis.  The Na content can reach as high as 1.63 per formula, which is the highest value for sodium iron hexacyanoferrate.  This Na-rich sodium iron hexacyanoferrate demonstrates a high specific capacity of 150 mAh·g-1 and remarkable cycling performance with 90% capacity retention after 200 cycles.  Furthermore, the Na intercalation/de-intercalation mechanism has been systematically studied by in situ Raman spectroscopy, X-ray diffraction and X-ray absorption spectroscopy anal. for the first time.  The Na-rich sodium iron hexacyanoferrate can function as a plenteous Na reservoir and has great potential as a cathode material for practical Na-ion batteries.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The development of electrodes for ambient temp. sodium-ion batteries requires the study of new materials and the understanding of how crystal structure affects properties.  In this study, where sodium is located in two Prussian blue analogs, Fe[Fe(CN)6]1-x·yH2O and FeCo(CN)6 is investigated.  The evolution of the sodium site occupancies, lattice and vol. is shown during charge-discharge using in situ synchrotron X-ray powder diffraction data.  Sodium insertion is found to occur in these electrodes during cell construction and therefore Fe[Fe(CN)6]1-x·yH2O and FeCo(CN)6 can be used as pos. electrodes. Its ability to promote diverse coordination chemistries in aq. media, in the presence of metal ions known to act as trace elements in human metab., earmarks its involvement in a no. of physiol. functions.  Co is known to be a central element of metabolically important biomols., such as B12, and therefore its biospeciation in biol. fluids constitutes a theme worthy of chem. and biol. perusal.  In an effort to unravel the aq. chem. of Co in the presence of a physiol. relevant ligand, citrate, the first aq. sol., mononuclear complex was synthesized and isolated from reaction mixts. contg. Corroborating this result is the EPR spectrum of 1, which shows a signal consistent with the presence of a Co(II) system. The sodium-ion concn. in the aq. soln. during pptn. has a strong influence on the compn. and structure of the final product, resulting in different reversible capacity and cycling performance.  With higher Na+ concn., a cooperative Na+ displacement along a single [111] direction induces a transition from cubic to rhombohedral symmetry - this phase transition is reversible on cycling the Na+ concn. Here we report high-quality NaxFeFe(CN)6 nanocubes as cathode materials for SIBs and investigate their sodium storage mechanism.  Among them, Na-rich Na1.70FeFe(CN)6 shows highly reversible electrochem. reactions, delivering a capacity as high as 120.7 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 200 mA g-1; even at 1200 mA g-1, the capacity still retains up to 73.6 mA h g-1.  The first-cycle coulombic efficiency strongly depends on the sodium content in NaxFeFe(CN)6.  Exptl. results and first-principle calcns. demonstrate that sodium cations in the large cavities of PBs have a priority to occupy the 8c site, while in the Na-rich samples, Na+ ions can be pushed into other 24d site.  We believe that our findings can provide new insights into sodium storage mechanism for the PBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref37"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref37" class="refNumLink">37</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit37" data-doi="10.1021/ja105482k"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Matsuda, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kim, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Moritomo, Y.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Symmetry Switch of Cobalt Ferrocyanide Framework by Alkaline Cation Exchange</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.                 10.1021/ja512383b</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D137%26publication_year%3D2015%26pages%3D2658-2664%26journal%3DJ.%2BAm.%2BChem.%2BSoc.%26author%3DJ.%2BSong%26author%3DL.%2BWang%26author%3DY.%2BLu%26author%3DJ.%2BLiu%26author%3DB.%2BGuo%26author%3DP.%2BXiao%26author%3DJ.%2BJ.%2BLee%26author%3DX.%2BQ.%2BYang%26author%3DG.%2BHenkelman%26author%3DJ.%2BB.%2BGoodenough%26title%3DRemoval%2Bof%2BInterstitial%2BH2O%2Bin%2BHexacyanometallates%2Bfor%2Ba%2BSuperior%2BCathode%2Bof%2Ba%2BSodium-Ion%2BBattery%26doi%3D10.1021%252Fja512383b&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.5b12620&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Fja512383b&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit43R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">43</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Removal of Interstitial H2O in Hexacyanometallates for a Superior Cathode of a Sodium-Ion Battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Song, Jie; Wang, Long; Lu, Yuhao; Liu, Jue; Guo, Bingkun; Xiao, Penghao; Lee, Jong-Jan; Yang, Xiao-Qing; Henkelman, Graeme; Goodenough, John B.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">137</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2658-2664</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JACSAT</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0002-7863</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium is globally available, which makes a sodium-ion rechargeable battery preferable to a lithium-ion battery for large-scale storage of elec. energy, provided a host cathode for Na can be found that provides the necessary capacity, voltage, and cycle life at the prescribed charge/discharge rate.  Low-cost hexacyanometallates are promising cathodes because of their ease of synthesis and rigid open framework that enables fast Na+ insertion and extn.  Here an intriguing effect is reported of interstitial H2O on the structure and electrochem. properties of sodium manganese(II) hexacyanoferrates(II) with the nominal compn. Na2MnFe(CN)6·zH2O (Na2-δMnHFC).  The newly discovered dehydrated Na2-δMnHFC phase exhibits superior electrochem. performance compared to other reported Na-ion cathode materials; it delivers at 3.5 V a reversible capacity of 150 mAh g-1 in a sodium half cell and 140 mAh g-1 in a full cell with a hard-carbon anode.  At a charge/discharge rate of 20 C, the half-cell capacity is 120 mAh g-1, and at 0.7 C, the cell exhibits 75% capacity retention after 500 cycles.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></div><script type="text/javascript" nonce="931886b2fc4354b8-SJC">window.refViewer={'ref1':['cit1'],'ref2':['cit2'],'ref3':['cit3'],'ref4':['cit4'],'ref5':['cit5'],'ref6':['cit6'],'ref7':['cit7'],'ref8':['cit8'],'ref9':['cit9'],'ref10':['cit10'],'ref11':['cit11'],'ref12':['cit12'],'ref13':['cit13'],'ref14':['cit14'],'ref15':['cit15'],'ref16':['cit16'],'ref17':['cit17'],'ref18':['cit18'],'ref19':['cit19'],'ref20':['cit20'],'ref21':['cit21'],'ref22':['cit22'],'ref23':['cit23'],'ref24':['cit24'],'ref25':['cit25'],'ref26':['cit26'],'ref27':['cit27'],'ref28':['cit28'],'ref29':['cit29'],'ref30':['cit30'],'ref31':['cit31'],'ref32':['cit32'],'ref33':['cit33'],'ref34':['cit34'],'ref35':['cit35'],'ref36':['cit36'],'ref37':['cit37'],'ref38':['cit38'],'ref39':['cit39'],'ref40':['cit40'],'ref41':['cit41'],'ref42':['cit42'],'ref43':['cit43']}</script><div class="articleCitedByDropzone" data-pb-dropzone="articleCitedByDropzone"><div class="cited-by"><div class="article_content-header cited-content_header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div tabindex="0" class="article_content-title"><h2 id="citeThis">Cited By</h2><div class="article__copy"> <button class="article__copy__btn"><i aria-hidden="true" class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><div class="cited-by__content"><div class="sciteMetricsDropzone">

aria-labelledby="pane-m1" class="tab__pane tab__pane-figures active"><figure data-id="f_tgr1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Abstract</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#f_tgr1"><img class="rightTab-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/asset/images/medium/am-2015-12620u_0005.gif" alt="" id="rightTab-tgr1"></a><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/asset/images/large/am-2015-12620u_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.5b12620&amp;id=f_tgr1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="sch1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Scheme 1</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#sch1"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/asset/images/medium/am-2015-12620u_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/asset/images/large/am-2015-12620u_0004.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr1" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><div class="title2">Scheme 1. Schematic Crystal Structures of Prussian Blue Frameworks</div></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/asset/images/large/am-2015-12620u_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.5b12620&amp;id=sch1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div><p class="last"></p><p><span class="fn-label">Scheme a</span>An intact Na<sub>2</sub>M<sup>II</sup>[Fe<sup>II</sup>(CN)<sub>6</sub>] framework without structural defects. The M ions are 6-fold coordinated to the nitrogen atoms of the CN ligands, whereas Fe ions are octahedrally neighbored with the carbon atoms of the CN ligands, forming a three-dimensional polymeric framework with large interstitial spaces. The guests (Na<sup>+</sup> ions and zeolitic water molecules) occupy the interstitial “A” sites at the center of every eight sub-cubes. Zeolitic water molecules are omitted for clarity.</p><p class="last"><span class="fn-label">Scheme b</span>An ideally defective NaM<sup>II</sup>[Fe<sup>II</sup>(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.75</sub>•<sub>□0</sub>.<sub>25</sub> framework with 25% Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> vacancies existing in each unit cell. In this case, The M ions neighboring to vacancies are coordinated by water molecules whose total number in the unit is six. A real Prussian blue product would fall in between these two ideal crystal structures depending on the synthesis route.</p><p></p></figure><figure data-id="fig1" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig1"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/asset/images/medium/am-2015-12620u_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/asset/images/large/am-2015-12620u_0007.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr2" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Schematic illustration of the conventional coprecipitation method (I) and the citrate-assisted controlled crystallization process (II) for the synthesis of Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub>; (b) and (c) UV–vis absorption spectra of citrate, Co<sup>2+</sup>, citrate–Co<sup>2+</sup>, Na<sub>4</sub>Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub>; (d) In situ UV–vis spectral monitoring of the crystallization kinetics of Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> synthesized with or without the addition of citrate ions as slow-release chelator. The absorption intensity is recorded at 370 nm, which is characteristic of Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub>. The inset is a magnified picture of the initial nucleation stage.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/asset/images/large/am-2015-12620u_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.5b12620&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig2" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig2"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/asset/images/medium/am-2015-12620u_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/asset/images/large/am-2015-12620u_0001.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr3" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Physical properties of the as-prepared Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> sample: (a) XRD patterns; (b) crystal structure; (c) SEM image; and (d and e) energy dispersive spectroscopy mapping images.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/asset/images/large/am-2015-12620u_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.5b12620&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig3" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig3"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/asset/images/medium/am-2015-12620u_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/asset/images/large/am-2015-12620u_0002.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr4" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Electrochemical performance of the Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> electrode: (a) typical CV curve measured at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>; (b) charge–discharge profiles at a current density of 10 mA g<sup>–1</sup>; (c) rate performance; and (d) long-term cycle performance at current density of 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/asset/images/large/am-2015-12620u_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.5b12620&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig4" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig4"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/asset/images/medium/am-2015-12620u_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/asset/images/large/am-2015-12620u_0003.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr5" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The increasing interest in energy storage for the grid can be attributed to multiple factors, including the capital costs of managing peak demands, the investments needed for grid reliability, and the integration of renewable energy sources.  Although existing energy storage is dominated by pumped hydroelec., there is the recognition that battery systems can offer a no. of high-value opportunities, provided that lower costs can be obtained.  The battery systems reviewed here include sodium-sulfur batteries that are com. available for grid applications, redox-flow batteries that offer low cost, and lithium-ion batteries whose development for com. electronics and elec. vehicles is being applied to grid storage.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref2" class="rightTabRefNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit2" data-doi="10.1038/451652a"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Armand, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tarascon, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Energy prodn. and storage have become key issues concerning our welfare in daily life.  Present challenges for batteries are twofold.  In the first place, the increasing demand for powering systems of portable electronic devices and zero-emission vehicles stimulates research towards high energy and high voltage systems.  In the second place, low cost batteries are required in order to advance towards smart elec. grids that integrate discontinuous energy flow from renewable sources, optimizing the performance of clean energy sources.  Na-ion batteries can be the key for the second point, because of the huge availability of sodium, its low price and the similarity of both Li and Na insertion chemistries.  In spite of the lower energy d. and voltage of Na-ion based technologies, they can be focused on applications where the wt. and footprint requirement is less drastic, such as elec. grid storage.  Much work has to be done in the field of Na-ion in order to catch up with Li-ion technol.  Cathodic and anodic materials must be optimized, and new electrolytes will be the key point for Na-ion success.  This review will gather the up-to-date knowledge about Na-ion battery materials, with the aim of providing a wide view of the systems that have already been explored and a starting point for the new research on this battery technol.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref4" class="rightTabRefNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit4" data-doi="10.1039/c3ee40847g"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Pan, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, Y.-S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, L.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Room-Temperature Stationary Sodium-Ion Batteries for Large-Scale Electric Energy Storage</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Energy Environ.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries have attracted great attention particularly in large-scale elec. energy storage applications for renewable energy and smart grid because of the huge abundant sodium resources and low cost.  In this article, a variety of electrode materials including cathodes and anodes as well as electrolytes for room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries are briefly reviewed.  We compare the difference in storage behavior between Na and Li in their analogous electrodes and summarize the sodium storage mechanisms in the available electrode materials.  This review also includes some new results from our group and our thoughts on developing new materials.  Some perspectives and directions on designing better materials for practical applications are pointed out based on knowledge from the literature and our experience.  Through this extensive literature review, the search for suitable electrode and electrolyte materials for stationary sodium-ion batteries is still challenging.  However, after intensive research efforts, we believe that low-cost, long-life and room-temp. sodium-ion batteries would be promising for applications in large-scale energy storage system in the near future.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref5" class="rightTabRefNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit5" data-doi="10.1021/cr500192f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yabuuchi, N.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kubota, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dahbi, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Komaba, S.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Research Development on Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Chem.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered a top alternative to Li-ion batteries (LIBs) for large-scale renewable energy storage units due to their low cost and the abundance of Na-bearing precursors in the earth's mineral deposits.  However, the development of anode materials for SIBs to date was mainly limited to carbonaceous materials with minimal research devoted to high capacity alloy-based materials.  An Sb/C electrode with ∼30 nm Sb nanoparticles (NPs) uniformly encapsulated in interconnecting 1-dimensional (1D) 400 nm C fibers (denoted as SbNP@C) was fabricated using a simple and scalable electrospinning method.  This binder-free, current collector-free SbNP@C electrode demonstrated high capacity and stable long-term cycling performance at various current densities.  The SbNP@C electrode showed an initial total capacity of 422 mA-h/gelectrode and retained 350 mA-h/gelectrode after 300 deep charge-discharge cycles under 100 mA/gSb.  Also, because of the efficient 1-dimensional Na-ion transport pathway and the highly conductive network of SbNP@C, the electrode preserved high overall capacities even when cycled at high currents, extending its usability to high power applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref8" class="rightTabRefNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit8" data-doi="10.1038/nmat3309"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yabuuchi, N.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kajiyama, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Iwatate, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Nishikawa, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hitomi, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Okuyama, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Usui, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yamada, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Komaba, S.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">P2-Type Na<sub>x</sub>[Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> Made from Earth-Abundant Elements for Rechargeable Na batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Nat.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Rechargeable Li batteries have risen to prominence as key devices for green and sustainable energy development.  Mn- and Fe-based cathode materials, such as LiMn2O4 and LiFePO4, are used in large-scale batteries for elec. vehicles.  Mn and Fe are abundant elements in the Earth's crust, but Li is not. Herein, we introduce a novel cathode material, Na1.5VPO4.8F0.7, for Na-ion batteries.  This new material provides an energy d. of ∼600 Wh kg-1, the highest value among cathodes, originating from both the multielectron redox reaction (1.2 e- per formula unit) and the high potential (∼3.8 V vs Na+/Na) of the tailored vanadium redox couple (V3.8+/V5+). The open crystal framework with two-dimensional Na diffusional pathways leads to low activation barriers for Na diffusion, enabling excellent rate capability. Copper hexacyanoferrate and nickel hexacyanoferrate, two open framework materials with the Prussian Blue structure, were recently shown to offer ultralong cycle life and high-rate performance when operated as battery electrodes in safe, inexpensive aq. sodium ion and potassium ion electrolytes.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Owing to the worldwide abundance and low-cost of Na, room-temp. Na-ion batteries are emerging as attractive energy storage systems for large-scale grids. Prussian blue and its analogs (PBAs) are promising Na-rich cathode materials since they can theor. store two Na+ ions per formula unit.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The development of electrodes for ambient temp. sodium-ion batteries requires the study of new materials and the understanding of how crystal structure affects properties.  In this study, where sodium is located in two Prussian blue analogs, Fe[Fe(CN)6]1-x·yH2O and FeCo(CN)6 is investigated.  The evolution of the sodium site occupancies, lattice and vol. is shown during charge-discharge using in situ synchrotron X-ray powder diffraction data.  Sodium insertion is found to occur in these electrodes during cell construction and therefore Fe[Fe(CN)6]1-x·yH2O and FeCo(CN)6 can be used as pos. electrodes.  NazFeFe(CN)6 electrodes feature higher reversible capacities relative to NazFeCo(CN)6 electrodes which can be assocd. with a combination of structural factors, for example, a major sodium-contg. phase, ∼Na0.5FeFe(CN)6 with sodium locating either at the x = y = z = 0.25 or x = y = 0.25, and z = 0.227(11) sites and an electrochem. inactive sodium-free Fe[Fe(CN)6]1-x·yH2O phase.         (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">137</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2548-2554</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JACSAT</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0002-7863</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A novel air-stable sodium iron hexacyanoferrate (R-Na1.92Fe[Fe(CN)6]) with rhombohedral structure is demonstrated to be a scalable, low-cost cathode material for sodium-ion batteries exhibiting high capacity, long cycle life, and good rate capability.  The cycling mechanism of the iron redox is clarified and understood through synchrotron-based soft x-ray absorption spectroscopy, which also reveals the correlation between the phys. properties and the cell performance of this novel material.  More importantly, successful prepn. of a dehydrated iron hexacyanoferrate with high sodium-ion concn. enables the fabrication of a discharged sodium-ion battery with a non-sodium metal anode, and the manufg. feasibility of low cost sodium-ion batteries with existing lithium-ion battery infrastructures has been tested.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref40"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref40" class="rightTabRefNumLink">40</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit40" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201100655"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Qian, J. F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhou, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cao, Y. L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ai, X. P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, H. X.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Nanosized Na<sub>4</sub>Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>/C Composite as a Low-Cost and High-Rate Cathode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv. Energy Mater.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2012</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">2</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">410</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">414</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/aenm.201100655</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit40R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">40</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Nanosized Na4Fe(CN)6/C composite as a low-cost and high-rate cathode material for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Qian, Jianfeng; Zhou, Min; Cao, Yuliang; Ai, Xinping; Yang, Hanxi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">2</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">410-414</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADEMBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1614-6840</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A Na4Fe(CN)6/C nanocomposite with exceptional cycling stability and high rate capability as for Na-ion batteries was synthesized.  This Na4Fe(CN)6/C nanocomposite can deliver its theor. one-electron redox capacity of -90 mAh/g and realize &gt;60% of its initial capacity at a very high rate of 8C (-720 mA/g), possibly serving as a low-cost and pollution-free cathode of Na-ion batteries for large-scale elec. energy storage applications.  Particularly, the environmental friendliness and low cost of the cathodic material enable it to be used for large-scale elec. storage applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref41"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref41" class="rightTabRefNumLink">41</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit41" data-doi="10.7567/APEX.6.025802"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Takachi, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Matsuda, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Moritomo, Y.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Cobalt Hexacyanoferrate as Cathode Material for Na<sup>+</sup> Secondary Battery</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Appl. Phys. Express</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2013</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">6</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">025802</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.7567/APEX.6.025802</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit41R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">41</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Cobalt hexacyanoferrate as cathode material for Na+ secondary battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Takachi, Masamitsu; Matsuda, Tomoyuki; Moritomo, Yutaka</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Applied Physics Express</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">025802/1-025802/3</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">APEPC4</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1882-0778</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Japan Society of Applied Physics</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">We investigated structural and electrochem. properties of thin film electrodes of cobalt hexacyanoferrate, NaxCo[Fe(CN)6]0.902.9H2O, against x.  The compd. exhibits a high capacity of 135 mAh/g and an av. operating voltage of 3.6 V against Na, with a good cyclability.  The discharge curve exhibits two plateaus at ≈ 3.8 and ≈ 3.4 V, which are ascribed to the redn. processes of Fe3+ and Co3+, resp.  The ex situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) profiles reveal the robust nature of the host framework against Na+ intercalation/deintercalation.  Thus, cobalt hexacyanoferrate is a promising candidate for the cathode material of sodium-ion secondary battery (SIB).</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref42"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref42" class="rightTabRefNumLink">42</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit42" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2015.02.019"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, W.-J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chou, S.-L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, J.-Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, J.-L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Gu, Q.-F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, H.-K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dou, S.-X.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Multifunctional Conducing Polymer Coated Na<sub>1+x</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> Cathode for Sodium-Ion Batteries with Superior Performance Via a Facile and One-Step Chemistry Approach</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Nano Energy</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">13</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">200</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">207</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.nanoen.2015.02.019</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit42R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">42</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Multifunctional conducing polymer coated Na1+xMnFe(CN)6 cathode for sodium-ion batteries with superior performance via a facile and one-step chemistry approach</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Wei-Jie; Chou, Shu-Lei; Wang, Jia-Zhao; Wang, Jian-Li; Gu, Qin-Fen; Liu, Hua-Kun; Dou, Shi-Xue</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">13</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">200-207</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NEANCA</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2211-2855</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A facile, one-step, soft chem. approach is developed to synthesize ClO4-doped polypyrrole coated Na1+xMnFe(CN)6 composite as a cathode material (NMHFC@PPy) for SIBs.  PPy plays multiple important roles in the composite.  First, PPy serves as a conductive coating layer which can increase the electronic cond. of NMHFC to improve the rate capability.  Second, PPy can act as a protective layer to reduce the dissoln. of Mn in the electrolyte to improve the cycling performance.  Finally, the PPy doped with ClO-4 can act as active materials to increase the capacity of the composite.  NMHFC@PPy shows high energy d. (428 W h kg-1), enhanced cycling performance (67% capacity retention after 200 cycles), and excellent rate capacity (46% capacity for 40 C rate).</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref43"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref43" class="rightTabRefNumLink">43</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit43" data-doi="10.1021/ja512383b"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Song, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Guo, B.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xiao, P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lee, J. J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, X. Q.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Henkelman, G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Goodenough, J. B.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Removal of Interstitial H<sub>2</sub>O in Hexacyanometallates for a Superior Cathode of a Sodium-Ion Battery</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J. Am. Chem. Soc.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">137</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">2658</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">2664</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/ja512383b</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit43R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">43</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Removal of Interstitial H2O in Hexacyanometallates for a Superior Cathode of a Sodium-Ion Battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Song, Jie; Wang, Long; Lu, Yuhao; Liu, Jue; Guo, Bingkun; Xiao, Penghao; Lee, Jong-Jan; Yang, Xiao-Qing; Henkelman, Graeme; Goodenough, John B.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">137</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2658-2664</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JACSAT</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0002-7863</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium is globally available, which makes a sodium-ion rechargeable battery preferable to a lithium-ion battery for large-scale storage of elec. energy, provided a host cathode for Na can be found that provides the necessary capacity, voltage, and cycle life at the prescribed charge/discharge rate.  Low-cost hexacyanometallates are promising cathodes because of their ease of synthesis and rigid open framework that enables fast Na+ insertion and extn.  Here an intriguing effect is reported of interstitial H2O on the structure and electrochem. properties of sodium manganese(II) hexacyanoferrates(II) with the nominal compn. Na2MnFe(CN)6·zH2O (Na2-δMnHFC).  The newly discovered dehydrated Na2-δMnHFC phase exhibits superior electrochem. performance compared to other reported Na-ion cathode materials; it delivers at 3.5 V a reversible capacity of 150 mAh g-1 in a sodium half cell and 140 mAh g-1 in a full cell with a hard-carbon anode.  At a charge/discharge rate of 20 C, the half-cell capacity is 120 mAh g-1, and at 0.7 C, the cell exhibits 75% capacity retention after 500 cycles.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li><li id="pane-m3" aria-labelledby="pane-m3" class="tab__pane tab__pane-suppInfo"><h2 class="supp-info-label">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article_supporting-info" id="rightTab-notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i9">Supporting Information</h2></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.5b12620" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.5b12620</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Chemical compositions, TG curves, infrared spectra, XRD patterns, SEM images, electrochemical properties of these two Na<sub>2</sub>CoFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> materials. (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/suppl_file/am5b12620_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b12620/suppl_file/am5b12620_si_001.pdf">am5b12620_si_001.pdf (1.33 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div><div id="rightTab-figshare-widget"></div><br><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="b84e6b6c-b4d0-4427-b286-3cf14d52d013" class="mb-4">

	//	e.preventDefault();

	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	}); 

 	$('.header_contnav-pre a').on('click', function(e){
	    e.preventDefault();
	     gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Left Arrow'});
	}); 

	$('.share_button').on('click', function(e){
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Share'});
	});

	$("#pane-m1con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Figures'});
	});

	$("#pane-m2con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'References'});
	});

	$("#pane-m3con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Supporting Info'});
	});

	$('.tab__close').on('click', function(){
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Close'});
	}); 



})();

</script>

<script nonce="931886b2fc4354b8-SJC">
document.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', function () {
    const modalContainer = document.querySelector('.my-main-container');

    // Function to show the modal
    function showModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'flex';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'block';
    }

    // Function to hide the modal when the close button is clicked
    function hideModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'none';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'none';
    }

    // Hide the modal initially
    hideModal();

    // Function to handle clicks outside the modal
    function handleClickOutside(event) {
        // const modalContainer = document.querySelector(".my-main-container");
        // Check if the click target is not a child of the modal container
        if (!modalContainer.contains(event.target)) {
            hideModal();
        }
    }

    // Add a click event listener to the close button
    document
        .querySelector('.close-button')
        .addEventListener('click', hideModal);

    // Add a click event listener to the document to handle clicks outside the modal
    document.addEventListener('click', handleClickOutside);

    const screenHeight = window.innerHeight;
    function updateModalPosition() {
        const scrollY = window.scrollY;
        // console.log("scrollY= " + scrollY);
        // modalContainer.style.top = `${50 + scrollY}px`;
        modalContainer.style.top = `${scrollY + screenHeight / 2}px`;
        // console.log("new top= " + modalContainer.style.top);
    }

    // Update the modal position when the user scrolls
    window.addEventListener('scroll', updateModalPosition);

    // Initialize the modal position
    updateModalPosition();

    //---Cookie---
    // Function to set the cookie with a given name, value, and expiration time in days
    function setCookie(name, value, days) {
        var expires = '';
        if (days) {
            var date = new Date();
            date.setTime(date.getTime() + days * 24 * 60 * 60 * 1000);
            expires = '; expires=' + date.toUTCString();
            console.log(expires + 'this is expires dateUTC');
        }
        document.cookie = name + '=' + (value || '') + expires + '; path=/';
        // console.log("document.cookie is: " + document.cookie);
    }

    if (!document.cookie.includes('modalAd')) {
        // Show Modal
        showModal();

        // Set the cookie with the name "modalAd", value "true", and expiration of 3 day
        setCookie('modalAd', 'true', 3);
    } else {
        hideModal();
    }
});

</script>

        </div>

<script nonce="931886b2fc4354b8-SJC">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

      function init() {
        setTimeout(function () {
          var cookieExist = getCookie("gdpr");
          if (cookieExist) {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie exist");
            return false;
          } else {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie does not exist")
            gdprMessage();

          //Handle submit
          $("#gdpr-message-btn").on("click", function (event) {
            handleClick();

        //console.log("Show GDPR Message");
        var tMessage, emailInput, messageButton, ewrap;

        $("#gdpr-message-modal").show();
      }

      function handleClick() {
        //console.log($("#gdpr-input").val());
        emailValid = validateEmail($("#gdpr-input").val());
        if (emailValid) {
          //Submit the form
          //Hide form
          if ($(".gdpr_err")) {
            $(".gdpr_err").hide();
          }

          $("#gdpr-message-content");
          //If successful then...
          setCookie("gdpr", "true");
          //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid + " submiting form");

          //Show error message
          gdprErrorMessage();
          //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid);
        }
      }

      function gdprErrorMessage() {
        if (errorStatus) {
          return false;
        } else {
          errorStatus = true;
          $("#gdpr-message-content").append(errormessage);
        }
      }

      function validateEmail(email) {
        var re =
          /^(([^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+(\.[^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+)*)|(".+"))@((\[[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\])|(([a-zA-Z\-0-9]+\.)+[a-zA-Z]{2,}))$/;
        return re.test(String(email).toLowerCase());
      }

      function setCookie(name, value) {
        var expires = "";
        expires = "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT";
        document.cookie = name + "=" + (value || "") + expires + "; path=/";
      }

      function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + "=([^;]+)"));
        if (match) return match[1];
      }

      function hideForm() {
        //And set cookie
        $("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
        setCookie("gdpr", "false");
        gtag("event", "GDPR Modal", {
          event_category: "Pubs GDPR",
          event_label: "GDPR Dismissal",
        });
      }

      return {
        init: init,
      };
    })();

  </script>

        <script nonce="931886b2fc4354b8-SJC">
var currentJournal = "ACS Applied Materials &amp;amp; Interfaces";
$(".citationLinks > a").click(function(e){
    if($(this).text() === "ACS Full Text ") {
        gtag('event', 'ACS Full Text', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Crossref") {
        gtag('event', 'Crossref', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "CAS") {
        gtag('event', 'CAS', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "PubMed") {
        gtag('event', 'PubMed', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Google Scholar") {
        gtag('event', 'Google Scholar', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).hasClass("issue-item_open-url") === true) {
        gtag('event', 'OpenURL', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
});
</script>
